        <p style="font-size: calc(1em + ((0.4vw + 0.25vh) / 2));"> <strong>You know, we use ad-blockers as well.</strong> We gotta keep those servers running though.        <article class="post-116016 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail hentry category-coding tag-career tag-javascript" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="TechArticle"> 
        <h2> <a property="url" href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/" rel="bookmark" title="Read '7 JavaScript Things I Wish I Knew Much Earlier In My Career'"><span property="name">7 JavaScript Things I Wish I Knew Much Earlier In My Career</span></a> </h2> 
        <ul class="pmd clearfix"> 
         <li class="a" property="author" typeof="person" resource="#authorname">By <a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/author/christian-heilmann/" title="Posts by Christian Heilmann" rel="author">Christian Heilmann</a></li> 
         <li class="rd" property="datePublished" content="April 20th, 2010">April 20th, 2010</li> 
         <li class="tags" property="keywords"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/tag/career/">Career</a><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/tag/javascript/">JavaScript</a></li> 
         <li class="comments" property="discussionUrl"><a href="#comments" title="Comment on 7 JavaScript Things I Wish I Knew Much Earlier In My Career">146 Comments</a> </li> 
        </ul> 
        <div class="ad ed"> 
         <div class="oa_zone--ad cad" id="cad" data-ad-name="Medium Rectangle" data-ad-zone="22" data-ad-media="all"></div> 
        </div> 
        <p>I’ve been writing JavaScript code for much longer than I care to remember. I am very excited about the language’s recent success; it’s good to be a part of that success story. I’ve written dozens of articles, book chapters and one full book on the matter, and yet I keep finding new things. Here are some of the “aha!” moments I’ve had in the past, which you can try out rather than waiting for them to come to you by chance. [Links checked February/15/2017]</p> 
        <h3 id="further-reading-on-smashingmag"><span class="rh">Further Reading</span> on SmashingMag: <a href="#further-reading-on-smashingmag" aria-label="Link to section 'Further Reading on SmashingMag:'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <ul> 
         <li><a title="Read 'The Seven Deadly Sins Of JavaScript Implementation'" href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/02/the-seven-deadly-sins-of-javascript-implementation/" rel="bookmark">The Seven Deadly Sins Of JavaScript Implementation</a><sup class="po" id="note-1"><a href="#1">1</a></sup></li> 
         <li><a title="Read 'Designing Better JavaScript APIs'" href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2012/10/designing-javascript-apis-usability/" rel="bookmark">Designing Better JavaScript APIs</a><sup class="po" id="note-2"><a href="#2">2</a></sup></li> 
         <li><a title="Read 'Writing Fast, Memory-Efficient JavaScript'" href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2012/11/writing-fast-memory-efficient-javascript/" rel="bookmark"><span class="headline">Writing Fast, Memory-Efficient JavaScript</span></a><sup class="po" id="note-3"><a href="#3">3</a></sup></li> 
         <li><a title="Read 'Useful JavaScript Libraries and jQuery Plugins — Part 2'" href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/useful-javascript-libraries-jquery-plugins-part-2/" rel="bookmark">Useful JavaScript Libraries and jQuery Plugins </a><sup class="po" id="note-4"><a href="#4">4</a></sup></li> 
        </ul> 
        <h3 id="shortcut-notations">Shortcut Notations <a href="#shortcut-notations" aria-label="Link to section 'Shortcut Notations'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <p>One of the things I love most about JavaScript now is shortcut notations to generate objects and arrays. So, in the past when we wanted to create an object, we wrote:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var car = new Object();
car.colour = 'red';
car.wheels = 4;
car.hubcaps = 'spinning';
car.age = 4;</code></pre> 
        <p>The same can be achieved with:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var car = {
  colour:'red',
  wheels:4,
  hubcaps:'spinning',
  age:4
}</code></pre> 
        <p>Much shorter, and you don’t need to repeat the name of the object. Right now, <code>car</code> is fine, but what happens when you use <code>invalidUserInSession</code>? The main gotcha in this notation is IE. Never ever leave a trailing comma before the closing curly brace or you’ll be in trouble.</p> 
        <p>The other handy shortcut notation is for arrays. The old school way of defining arrays was this:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var moviesThatNeedBetterWriters = new Array(
  'Transformers','Transformers2','Avatar','Indiana Jones 4'
);</code></pre> 
        <p>The shorter version of this is:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var moviesThatNeedBetterWriters = [
  'Transformers','Transformers2','Avatar','Indiana Jones 4'
];</code></pre> 
        <p>The other thing about arrays is that there is no such thing as an associative array. You will find a lot of code examples that define the above <code>car</code> example like so:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var car = new Array();
car['colour'] = 'red';
car['wheels'] = 4;
car['hubcaps'] = 'spinning';
car['age'] = 4;</code></pre> 
        <p>This is not Sparta; this is madness—don’t bother with this. “Associative arrays” is a confusing name for objects.</p> 
        <p>Another very cool shortcut notation is the ternary notation for conditions. So, instead of the following…</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var direction;
if(x &lt; 200){
  direction = 1;
} else {
  direction = -1;
}</code></pre> 
        <p>… You could write a shorter version using the ternary notation:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var direction = x &lt; 200 ? 1 : -1;</code></pre> 
        <p>The <code>true</code> case of the condition is after the question mark, and the other case follows the colon.</p> 
        <section> 
         <div class="oa_zone--ad icad" id="cad-middle" data-ad-name="Content Ad Middle" data-ad-zone="110" data-ad-media="all"></div> 
        </section> 
        <h3 id="json-as-a-data-format">JSON As A Data Format <a href="#json-as-a-data-format" aria-label="Link to section 'JSON As A Data Format'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <p>Before I discovered JSON to store data, I did all kinds of crazy things to put content in a JavaScript-ready format: arrays, strings with control characters to split, and other abominations. The creation of <a href="http://json.org">JSON</a><sup class="po" id="note-5"><a href="#5">5</a></sup> by Douglas Crockford changed all that. Using JSON, you can store complex data in a format that is native to JavaScript and doesn’t need any extra conversion to be used immediately.</p> 
        <p>JSON is short for “JavaScript Object Notation” and uses both of the shortcuts we covered earlier.</p> 
        <p>So, if I wanted to describe a band, for example, I could do the following:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var band = {
  "name":"The Red Hot Chili Peppers",
  "members":[
    {
      "name":"Anthony Kiedis",
      "role":"lead vocals"
    },
    {
      "name":"Michael 'Flea' Balzary",
      "role":"bass guitar, trumpet, backing vocals"
    }, 
    {
      "name":"Chad Smith",
      "role":"drums,percussion"
    },
    {
      "name":"John Frusciante",
      "role":"Lead Guitar"
    }
  ],
  "year":"2009"
}</code></pre> 
        <p>You can use JSON directly in JavaScript and, when wrapped in a function call, even as a return format of APIs. This is called JSON-P and is supported by a lot of APIs out there. You can use a data endpoint, returning JSON-P directly in a script node:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">&lt;div id="delicious"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;script&gt;
function delicious(o){
  var out = '&lt;ul&gt;';
  for(var i=0;i&lt;o.length;i++){
    out += '&lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="' + o[i].u + '"&gt;' + 
           o[i].d + '&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;';
  }
  out += '&lt;/ul&gt;';
  document.getElementById('delicious').innerHTML = out;
}
&lt;/script&gt;
&lt;script src="http://feeds.delicious.com/v2/json/codepo8/javascript?count=15&amp;callback=delicious"&gt;&lt;/script&gt;</code></pre> 
        <p>This calls the Delicious Web service to get my latest JavaScript bookmarks in JSON format and then displays them as an unordered list.</p> 
        <p>In essence, JSON is probably the most lightweight way of describing complex data—and it runs in a browser. You can even use it in PHP using the <code>json_decode()</code> function.</p> 
        <h3 id="native-javascript-functions-math-array-and-string">Native JavaScript Functions (Math, Array And String) <a href="#native-javascript-functions-math-array-and-string" aria-label="Link to section 'Native JavaScript Functions (Math, Array And String)'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <p>One thing that amazed me is how much easier my life got once I read up thoroughly on the math and string functions of JavaScript. You can use these to avoid a lot of looping and conditions. For example, when I had the task of finding the largest number in an array of numbers, I used to write a loop, like so:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var numbers = [3,342,23,22,124];
var max = 0;
for(var i=0;i&lt;numbers.length;i++){
  if(numbers[i] &gt; max){
    max = numbers[i];
  }
}
alert(max);</code></pre> 
        <p>This can be achieved without a loop:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var numbers = [3,342,23,22,124];
numbers.sort(function(a,b){return b - a});
alert(numbers[0]);</code></pre> 
        <p>Notice that you cannot use <code>sort()</code> on a number array because it sorts lexically. There’s a <a href="http://www.javascriptkit.com/javatutors/arraysort.shtml">good tutorial on <code>sort()</code> here</a><sup class="po" id="note-6"><a href="#6">6</a></sup> in case you need to know more.</p> 
        <p>Another interesting method is <code>Math.max()</code>. This one returns the largest number from a list of parameters:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">Math.max(12,123,3,2,433,4); // returns 433</code></pre> 
        <p>Because this tests for numbers and returns the largest one, you can use it to test for browser support of certain properties:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var scrollTop= Math.max(
 doc.documentElement.scrollTop,
 doc.body.scrollTop
);</code></pre> 
        <p>This works around an Internet Explorer problem. You can read out the <code>scrollTop</code> of the current document, but depending on the <code>DOCTYPE</code> of the document, one or the other property is assigned the value. When you use <code>Math.max()</code> you get the right number because only one of the properties returns one; the other will be <code>undefined</code>. You can read more about shortening JavaScript with math functions <a href="http://www.wait-till-i.com/2007/06/28/shortening-javascripts-with-math/">here</a><sup class="po" id="note-7"><a href="#7">7</a></sup>.</p> 
        <p>Other very powerful functions to manipulate strings are <code>split()</code> and <code>join()</code>. Probably the most powerful example of this is writing a function to attach CSS classes to elements.</p> 
        <p>The thing is, when you add a class to a DOM element, you want to add it either as the first class or to already existing classes with a space in front of it. When you remove classes, you also need to remove the spaces (which was much more important in the past when some browsers failed to apply classes with trailing spaces).</p> 
        <p>So, the original function would be something like:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">function addclass(elm,newclass){
  var c = elm.className;
  elm.className = (c === '') ? newclass : c+' '+newclass;
}</code></pre> 
        <p>You can automate this using the <code>split()</code> and <code>join()</code> methods:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">function addclass(elm,newclass){
  var classes = elm.className.split(' ');
  classes.push(newclass);
  elm.className = classes.join(' ');
}</code></pre> 
        <p>This automatically ensures that classes are space-separated and that yours gets tacked on at the end.</p> 
        <h3 id="event-delegation">Event Delegation <a href="#event-delegation" aria-label="Link to section 'Event Delegation'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <p>Events make Web apps work. I love events, especially custom events, which make your products extensible without your needing to touch the core code. The main problem (and actually one of its strengths) is that events are removed from the HTML—you apply an event listener to a certain element and then it becomes active. Nothing in the HTML indicates that this is the case though. Take this abstraction issue (which is hard for beginners to wrap their heads around) and the fact that “browsers” such as IE6 have all kind of memory problems and too many events applied to them, and you’ll see that not using too many event handlers in a document is wise.</p> 
        <p>This is where <span class="removed_link" title="http://icant.co.uk/sandbox/eventdelegation/">event delegation</span> comes in. When an event happens on a certain element and on all the elements above it in the DOM hierarchy, you can simplify your event handling by using a single handler on a parent element, rather than using a lot of handlers.</p> 
        <p>What do I mean by that? Say you want a list of links, and you want to call a function rather than load the links. The HTML would be:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">&lt;h2&gt;Great Web resources&lt;/h2&gt;
&lt;ul id="resources"&gt;
  &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="http://opera.com/wsc"&gt;Opera Web Standards Curriculum&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;
  &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="http://sitepoint.com"&gt;Sitepoint&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;
  &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="http://alistapart.com"&gt;A List Apart&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;
  &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="http://yuiblog.com"&gt;YUI Blog&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;
  &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="http://blameitonthevoices.com"&gt;Blame it on the voices&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;
  &lt;li&gt;&lt;a href="http://oddlyspecific.com"&gt;Oddly specific&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/li&gt;
&lt;/ul&gt;</code></pre> 
        <p>The normal way to apply event handlers here would be to loop through the links:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">// Classic event handling example
(function(){
  var resources = document.getElementById('resources');
  var links = resources.getElementsByTagName('a');
  var all = links.length;
  for(var i=0;i&lt;all;i++){
    // Attach a listener to each link
    links[i].addEventListener('click',handler,false);
  };
  function handler(e){
    var x = e.target; // Get the link that was clicked
    alert(x);
    e.preventDefault();
  };
})();</code></pre> 
        <p>This could also be done with a single event handler:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">(function(){
  var resources = document.getElementById('resources');
  resources.addEventListener('click',handler,false);
  function handler(e){
    var x = e.target; // get the link tha
    if(x.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'a'){
      alert('Event delegation:' + x);
      e.preventDefault();
    }
  };
})();</code></pre> 
        <p>Because the click happens on all the elements in the list, all you need to do is compare the <code>nodeName</code> to the right element that you want to react to the event.</p> 
        <p>Disclaimer: while both of the event examples above work in browsers, they fail in IE6. For IE6, you need to apply an event model other than the W3C one, and this is why we use libraries for these tricks.</p> 
        <p>The benefits of this approach are more than just being able to use a single event handler. Say, for example, you want to add more links dynamically to this list. With event delegation, there is no need to change anything; with simple event handling, you would have to reassign handlers and re-loop the list.</p> 
        <h3 id="anonymous-functions-and-the-module-pattern">Anonymous Functions And The Module Pattern <a href="#anonymous-functions-and-the-module-pattern" aria-label="Link to section 'Anonymous Functions And The Module Pattern'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <p>One of the most annoying things about JavaScript is that it has no scope for variables. Any variable, function, array or object you define that is not inside another function is global, which means that other scripts on the same page can access—and will usually override— them.</p> 
        <p>The workaround is to encapsulate your variables in an anonymous function and call that function immediately after you define it. For example, the following definition would result in three global variables and two global functions:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var name = 'Chris';
var age = '34';
var status = 'single';
function createMember(){
  // [...]
}
function getMemberDetails(){
  // [...]
}</code></pre> 
        <p>Any other script on the page that has a variable named <code>status</code> could cause trouble. If we wrap all of this in a name such as <code>myApplication</code>, then we work around that issue:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var myApplication = function(){
  var name = 'Chris';
  var age = '34';
  var status = 'single';
  function createMember(){
    // [...]
  }
  function getMemberDetails(){
    // [...]
  }
}();</code></pre> 
        <p>This, however, doesn’t do anything outside of that function. If this is what you need, then great. You may as well discard the name then:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">(function(){
  var name = 'Chris';
  var age = '34';
  var status = 'single';
  function createMember(){
    // [...]
  }
  function getMemberDetails(){
    // [...]
  }
})();</code></pre> 
        <p>If you need to make some of the things reachable to the outside, then you need to change this. In order to reach <code>createMember()</code> or <code>getMemberDetails()</code>, you need to return them to the outside world to make them properties of <code>myApplication</code>:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var myApplication = function(){
  var name = 'Chris';
  var age = '34';
  var status = 'single';
  return{
    createMember:function(){
      // [...]
    },
    getMemberDetails:function(){
      // [...]
    }
  }
}();
// myApplication.createMember() and 
// myApplication.getMemberDetails() now works.</code></pre> 
        <p>This is called a module pattern or singleton. It was mentioned a lot by Douglas Crockford and is used very much in the <a href="https://yuilibrary.com/">Yahoo User Interface Library YUI</a><sup class="po" id="note-8"><a href="#8">8</a></sup>. What ails me about this is that I need to switch syntaxes to make functions or variables available to the outside world. Furthermore, if I want to call one method from another, I have to call it preceded by the <code>myApplication</code> name. So instead, I prefer simply to return pointers to the elements that I want to make public. This even allows me to shorten the names for outside use:</p> 
        <pre><code class="language-javascript">var myApplication = function(){
  var name = 'Chris';
  var age = '34';
  var status = 'single';
  function createMember(){
    // [...]
  }
  function getMemberDetails(){
    // [...]
  }
  return{
    create:createMember,
    get:getMemberDetails
  }
}();
//myApplication.get() and myApplication.create() now work.</code></pre> 
        <p>I’ve called this “<a href="http://www.wait-till-i.com/2007/08/22/again-with-the-module-pattern-reveal-something-to-the-world/">revealing module pattern</a><sup class="po" id="note-9"><a href="#9">9</a></sup>.”</p> 
        <h3 id="allowing-for-configuration">Allowing For Configuration <a href="#allowing-for-configuration" aria-label="Link to section 'Allowing For Configuration'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <p>Whenever I’ve written JavaScript and given it to the world, people have changed it, usually when they wanted it to do things that it couldn’t do out of the box—but also often because I made it too hard for people to change things.</p> 
        <p>The workaround is to add configuration objects to your scripts. I’ve <a href="http://www.wait-till-i.com/2008/05/23/script-configuration/">written about JavaScript configuration objects in detail</a><sup class="po" id="note-10"><a href="#10">10</a></sup>, but here’s the gist:</p> 
        <ul> 
         <li>Have an object as part of your whole script called <code>configuration</code>.</li> 
         <li>In it, store all of the things that people will likely change when they use your script: 
          <ul> 
           <li>CSS ID and class names;</li> 
           <li>Strings (such as labels) for generated buttons;</li> 
           <li>Values such as “number of images being displayed,” “dimensions of map”;</li> 
           <li>Location, locale and language settings.</li> 
          </ul> </li> 
         <li>Return the object as a public property so that people can override it.</li> 
        </ul> 
        <p>Most of the time you can do this as a last step in the coding process. I’ve put together an example in “<a href="http://www.wait-till-i.com/2008/02/07/five-things-to-do-to-a-script-before-handing-it-over-to-the-next-developer/">Five things to do to a script before handing it over to the next developer</a><sup class="po" id="note-11"><a href="#11">11</a></sup>.”</p> 
        <p>In essence, you want to make it easy for people to use your code and alter it to their needs. If you do that, you are much less likely to get confusing emails from people who complain about your scripts and refer to changes that someone else actually did.</p> 
        <h3 id="interacting-with-the-back-end">Interacting With The Back End <a href="#interacting-with-the-back-end" aria-label="Link to section 'Interacting With The Back End'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <p>One of the main things I learned from all my years with JavaScript is that it is a great language with which to make interactive interfaces, but when it comes to crunching numbers and accessing data sources, it can be daunting.</p> 
        <p>Originally, I learned JavaScript to replace Perl because I was sick of copying things to a <code>cgi-bin</code> folder in order to make it work. Later on, I learned that making a back-end language do the main data churning for me, instead of trying to do all in JavaScript, makes more sense with regard to security and language.</p> 
        <p>If I access a Web service, I could get JSON-P as the returned format and do a lot of data conversion on the client, but why should I when I have a server that has a richer way of converting data and that can return the data as JSON or HTML… and cache it for me to boot?</p> 
        <p>So, if you want to use AJAX, learn about HTTP and about writing your own caching and conversion proxy. You will save a lot of time and nerves in the long run.</p> 
        <h3 id="browser-specific-code-is-a-waste-of-time-use-libraries">Browser-Specific Code Is A Waste Of Time. Use Libraries! <a href="#browser-specific-code-is-a-waste-of-time-use-libraries" aria-label="Link to section 'Browser-Specific Code Is A Waste Of Time. Use Libraries!'" class="sr hsl">Link</a></h3> 
        <p>When I started Web development, the battle between using <code>document.all</code> and using <code>document.layers</code> as the main way to access the document was still raging. I chose <code>document.layers</code> because I liked the idea of any layer being its own document (and I had written more than enough <code>document.write</code> solutions to last a lifetime). The layer model failed, but so did <code>document.all</code>. When Netscape 6 went all out supporting only the W3C DOM model, I loved it, but end users didn’t care. End users just saw that this browser didn’t show the majority of the Internets correctly (although it did)—the code we produced was what was wrong. We built short-sighted code that supported a state-of-the-art environment, and the funny thing about the state of the art is that it is constantly changing.</p> 
        <p>I’ve wasted quite some time learning the ins and outs of all of the browsers and working around their issues. Doing this back then secured my career and ensured that I had a great job. But we shouldn’t have to go through this trial by fire any longer.</p> 
        <p>Libraries such as YUI, jQuery and Dojo are here to help us with this. They take on the problems of browsers by abstracting the pains of poor implementation, inconsistencies and flat-out bugs, and relieve us of the chore. Unless you want to beta test a certain browser because you’re a big fan, don’t fix browser issues in your JavaScript solutions, because you are unlikely to ever update the code to remove this fix. All you would be doing is adding to the already massive pile of outdated code on the Web.</p> 
        <p>That said, relying solely on libraries for your core skill is short-sighted. Read up on JavaScript, watch some good videos and tutorials on it, and understand the language. (Tip: closures are God’s gift to the JavaScript developer.) Libraries will help you build things quickly, but if you assign a lot of events and effects and need to add a class to every HTML element in the document, then you are doing it wrong.</p>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">8</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190359"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/6faae29f0d21676544ae89024b5c4b2f?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/6faae29f0d21676544ae89024b5c4b2f?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>sahil</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190359" title="Jump to Commentlink #8">April 20, 2010 7:14 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>Good article.</p> 
             <p>For the module pattern, you can also encapsulate your application in an object, instead of a function. So instead of</p> 
             <p><code><br> var myApplication = function(){<br> var name = 'Chris';<br> var age = '34';<br> var status = 'single';<br> function createMember(){<br> // [...]<br> }<br> function getMemberDetails(){<br> // [...]<br> }<br> }();<br> </code></p> 
             <p>we can have</p> 
             <p><code><br> var myApplication = {<br> name : 'Chris',<br> age : '34',<br> status : 'single',<br> createMember : function () {<br> // [...]<br> }<br> getMemberDetails : function (){<br> // [...]<br> }<br> }<br> </code></p> 
             <p>Of course, this does involve refactoring the code more but you don’t have to return functions.</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com"> 
             <div class="reply"></div> 
             <div class="rating__container clearfix" aria-live="polite">

          <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">11</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190366"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/0277dde1b6440039c4225b60d52e8ff5?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/0277dde1b6440039c4225b60d52e8ff5?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>James</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190366" title="Jump to Commentlink #11">April 20, 2010 8:11 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>I’m glad Christian has written another article for SM, but I’m afraid I have a couple of critical points to share.</p> 
             <p>Firstly, JSON is not the same as JavaScript’s object notation — sure, it’s a subset, but JSON is simply a data-interchange format. When you create an object literal and fill it with a bunch of properties you’re not creating JSON — that’s just an object. It’s quite a fuzzy topic, but I think you’ll find that there is some validity to my argument: <a href="http://benalman.com/news/2010/03/theres-no-such-thing-as-a-json/" rel="nofollow">http://benalman.com/news/2010/03/theres-no-such-thing-as-a-json/</a></p> 
             <p>I think it’s important to maintain a high standard when it comes to nomenclature. It’s not called “ternary notation”; it’s the “ternary operator”, or more correctly, the “conditional operator”. It happens to be the only operator in JS that accepts three operands, and that’s why it’s commonly referenced as the “ternary operator”. Also, the Math object is not a function, it’s a regular object that contains a bunch of “static” methods, like “abs”, “floor” and “max”.</p> 
             <p>I was surprised to see an entire section dedicated to Event Delegation, and not a single utterance of the words “bubbling” or “propagation” — If I was teaching Event Delegation I would start by explaining the fundamentals of DOM events, including how events bubble, and possibly discussing event capturing too.</p> 
             <p>Oh, also, finding the largest number in an array of numbers is EVEN simpler than you’re supposed ne plus ultra. E.g. Math.max.apply(null, [3,342,23,22,124]) === 342 ….</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com">

          <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">17</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190376"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/a0645ada8c57c337910f405883e61d2d?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/a0645ada8c57c337910f405883e61d2d?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>Arnoud ten Hoedt</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190376" title="Jump to Commentlink #17">April 20, 2010 11:23 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>A javascript technique I grabbed from the excellent Backbase architecture is to start solving crossbrowser CSS issues by adding classnames to your [html] tag based on the browser that is available.</p> 
             <p>So in firefox 3 you get [html class=”gecko gecko3?], while in internet explorer 8 you get [html class=”ie ie8?].<br> Such detection functions are easy to write and hook onload for any javascript framework, and provide you lots of additional gun powder when solving crossbrowser css issues.</p> 
             <p>And in the case of javascript not being available alltogether I still think you should nowadays consider to redirect users to a non-js/accessible/mobile skin of your website.</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com">

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">30</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190358"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/d64f4bc372c2b243c56b511cd31124d8?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/d64f4bc372c2b243c56b511cd31124d8?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>Jabe</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190358" title="Jump to Commentlink #30">April 20, 2010 7:13 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>Indeed, poor article. An example:</p> 
             <p>Take “Math.max(12,123,3,2,433,4)”, this piece of code is often useless since it uses a constant list of arguments. Most people would assume the max function also accepts an array, after all, this is JavaScript we are talking about. So try “Math.max([12,123,3,2,433,4])”, it returns NaN. This would be a great start to explain how functions work in JS: “Math.max.apply(null, [12,123,3,2,433,4])”.</p> 
             <p>Now THAT is some I wish I knew much earlier—not that Math.max exists.</p> 
             <p>The example before contradicts this. OK, it perfectly demonstrates the use of a lambda method to sort an array in descending order and pick the first element as “max” (BTW: what if the array is empty?), but why not simply use Math.max? Maybe because it does not accept arrays?</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com"> 
             <div class="reply"></div> 
             <div class="rating__container clearfix" aria-live="polite">

             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/bfd560be33eeb905e54c49bcebc2cbeb?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/bfd560be33eeb905e54c49bcebc2cbeb?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>hollsk</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190373" title="Jump to Commentlink #51">April 20, 2010 10:24 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>I’m guessing it happened when SM stopped posting crappy ‘inspiration’ posts and started posting things that people can have genuine professional opinions on. </p> 
             <p>Programming articles in particular attract a rather more robust breed of commenter. I’m actually all for it – if somebody thinks your programming knowledge could do with improvement, then nothing is gained by them keeping their trap shut about it. Sure, the “actually you’re wrong” posts might come across as dickish, but the geeks have never really been lauded for their social sensitivities. </p> 
             <p>For the noobs who get upset because “we’re not all good at it!”, do stop and think about whether best practice debate is something that could help you. Believe me, you won’t become good at it if you’re not willing to pay attention to the sources of conflicting advice where they occur.</p> 
             <p>Anyway, good article – but I wish everybody would stop using that fcking ternary operator everywhere, seriously. You can just about get away with it if it has a measurable impact on processing time, but for general usage just _don’t_. It is horrible for code clarity.</p> 
            </div>

             <p>I’m surprised at the comments saying that this is a simple beginner’s article. If you read it carefully, you will discover that it comments more deeply on topics that are normally in beginner’s articles.</p> 
             <p>I think the people moaning in the comments only looked at the topics, and did not read what the article had to say about those points. Consider the original material in the “revealing module pattern”.</p> 
             <p>That kind of new programming pattern is not a “hello world”. As a programmer of 30 years, I can say this is not a beginner’s article. It is simple, and it is deep.</p>

             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>“adding classnames to your [html] tag based on the browser that is available.”</p> 
             <p>Huh, that’s actually a pretty handy tip, thanks for posting that. </p> 
             <p>One thing to be aware of with it though is that IE6 (always with the IE6 &gt;:[ ) is hopeless with class chaining, only taking the final class in the chain to act upon – so it’d possibly be safe to assume IE6 for base structures and only dynamically chain classes for the modern browsers. </p> 
             <p>Good comment though, thanks again :)</p>

          <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">65</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190305"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/1cc8a55adb33d05bd8352f257adc7b0c?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/1cc8a55adb33d05bd8352f257adc7b0c?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>JeffG</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190305" title="Jump to Commentlink #65">April 20, 2010 3:41 pm</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>var numbers = [3,342,23,22,124];<br> numbers.sort(function(a,b){return b – a});<br> alert(numbers[0]);<br> —————————–<br> Yes, this works. However, it pays no attention to performance. This method will take orders of magnitude longer to execute than the “long” form that only loops over the array once.</p> 
             <p>Remember kids… fewer lines written does not necessarily mean better code. The author forgot how much is actually happening inside the sort() function. Just because you don’t see the code, doesn’t mean it isn’t there.</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com">

Maybe an icon or something visual representing the context would be good too.</p>

             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>Joe</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190405" title="Jump to Commentlink #80">April 21, 2010 7:56 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>I have been developing since the early 90’s and recently (last 7-8 years) got into the web development from application development. I would say that I like the articles I have seen, mostly…but lately there seems to have been a shortage. Anyone who thinks that a “basic” article is below them because of their experience is doomed to the stage they are, we learn by exploring and re-exploring…from time to time we need to check the facts we think we know.</p> 
             <p>Having said all that, I wanted to reply with some article requests. I know it can be difficult to provide such content, and ya’ll do a magnificent job of providing good content. </p> 
             <p>One topic I think would be very good has to do with database normalization in relation to the developer. If you read a lot of articles on the topic, you find this hard line of some developers saying normalization is worthless and others saying you need 4NF all the time. I think it would be good to see an article that went over normalization (in regard to the developer) and when it’s good as well as when you might look at denormalizing a structure…</p> 
             <p>I think I’d also like to see some articles on .Net development…there have been a lot of good additions to the languages over the past few years, and yet there are very few articles on some of the topics…although I’m not sure if I ever remember seeing a Smashing Magazine article on .Net development, so maybe it’s not in ya’lls lineup.</p> 
             <p>All that being said, I think the only thing I’d like to see from Smashing Magazine are more articles…I think what I have seen of your articles is good and I’d like to see more…</p> 
             <p>(Edit) Oh…and I think abstraction layers would be a great topic as well…not to mention development methodologies. There are a TON of new developers out there and many of them seem to think little of using standards or proper methodologies, etc…something to illustrate a few good techniques or something would be cool…explain how properly devised code structure can make implementation and management of the code during it’s lifetime a LOT easier…</p> 
             <p>Ok, sorry for the book-response…hasta</p>

             <p>Good artilce for a beginner like myself. I am as fresh (green) as they come right now and this was very helpful. Everything I’m reading right now either assumes I know too much and leaves out details I don’t know yet, or is waaay too simple (“hello world”) and uses doument.write for every stinkin example. Just an idea on another way to help beginners, coming from a beginner. </p> 
             <p>Thanks,<br> D</p> 
            </div>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">96</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190297"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/ac0748fc7fa669efb90eeb71f148ed1b?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/ac0748fc7fa669efb90eeb71f148ed1b?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>nfr</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190297" title="Jump to Commentlink #96">April 21, 2010 5:56 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>Using<br> —<br> var numbers = [3,342,23,22,124];<br> numbers.sort(function(a,b){return b – a});<br> alert(numbers[0]);<br> —<br> To get the maximum of an array this way is pure crazyness ; in terms of algorithmic complexity, you’re moving from O(n) to O(n.log(n)). That means that you’re basically multiplying by 10 (actually more, since the sort implementation always exceeds strict nlogn) the time needed to retrieve the maximum of a 1000-elements array.<br> Provided that usual sort implementations have a constant factor of about 25 to 35, it means that on a 1000-elements set, you’re multiplying the time needed to extract the max by 350. Great tip indeed!</p> 
             <p>I’m pretty disappointed that Smashing does such a mistake…</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com"> 
             <div class="reply"></div> 
             <div class="rating__container clearfix" aria-live="polite"> 
              <div id="rating__value--190297" class="rating__value">
               0
              </div> 
              <div class="rating__stars" data-comment_id="190297">

               <a class="rating__button rating__button--down" href="#" data-id="-1" data-nonce="914f00f273" title="Vote down!"></a>

            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>It seems that for every post on SM, some idiot simply has to make the “oh, this is waaaay too basic for me” comment.</p> 
             <p>Well, I appreciate the work SM and their contributors put into this great FREE resource for the design community. That’s right, COMMUNITY, as in for everyone, not just the handful of gurus out there.</p> 
             <p>Some of these self-appointed experts should perhaps contribute articles to this community rather than criticising others for doing so. Either that or simply stop reading articles that are obviously not aimed at them.</p> 
             <p>Keep up the great work SM!</p> 
            </div>

             <p>Quote: A return statement with a value should not use ( ) (parentheses) around the value. The return value expression must start on the same line as the return keyword in order to avoid semicolon insertion.</p>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">104</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190328"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/caaefeae15811188de49e613614c32ff?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/caaefeae15811188de49e613614c32ff?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>Resuna</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190328" title="Jump to Commentlink #104">April 21, 2010 8:09 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>Seconded! That was the first thing I noticed here. If your array is small, this use of sort may make sense (n * interpreted_code may be bigger than n * logn * compiled_code), but even a straightforward interpreter is likely to be no more than 50 times slower than the library sort, so for arrays that may be more than 2500 entries you’ll always be better off with a loop. If you’re using a JIT compiler, your interpreter overhead could be as low as 4 or 5 times, so stick to arrays less than 20 elements or so for the sort hack.</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com"> 
             <div class="reply"></div>

          <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">121</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190333"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/82ece4643b9e711b9ac6c3b4cb674622?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/82ece4643b9e711b9ac6c3b4cb674622?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>Amy Blankenship</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190333" title="Jump to Commentlink #121">April 23, 2010 7:09 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>“Much shorter, and you don’t need to repeat the name of the object. Right now, car is fine, but what happens when you use invalidUserInSession?”</p> 
             <p>Wouldn’t this work as well?</p> 
             <p>with (invalidUserInSession) {<br> prop1=’foo’;<br> prop2=’bar’;<br> }</p> 
             <p>I don’t believe your “singleton” pattern is any such thing–it doesn’t enforce a single instance of the variable. Instead, you’re taking advantage of ECMAScript method closures to pseudo encapsulate properties of an “object” that’s actually a function:</p> 
             <p><a href="http://jibbering.com/faq/faq_notes/closures.html" rel="nofollow">http://jibbering.com/faq/faq_notes/closures.html</a></p> 
             <p>HTH;</p> 
             <p>Amy</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com"> 
             <div class="reply"></div> 
             <div class="rating__container clearfix" aria-live="polite">

              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190345" title="Jump to Commentlink #131">April 27, 2010 8:41 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>Do you really often need to check for the current browser and Version? I have never used such hacks, in spite of the IE.</p> 
             <p>The problem will be, that it is much to easy to just write for known and not for future browsers. I don’t know where the problem with feature detection is. jQuery does it, MooTools has $defined(), it is much safer to use these constructs, because you can not accidently close the door for a future version of the same browser (e.g. IE9 with the W3C-Event Model).</p> 
            </div>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">132</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190410"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/3ea33ab59d369ffd6fa41cfe96ea20ca?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/3ea33ab59d369ffd6fa41cfe96ea20ca?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>Sara</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190410" title="Jump to Commentlink #132">April 29, 2010 1:02 pm</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>My own feeling about these javascript techniques as described in this article, is that many of them are exactly what is wrong with the language and what is wrong with a lot of C++ generation coding in general. The extreme terseness of many of these methods makes the code much more difficult to read and edit by a team of developers or by future developers.</p> 
             <p>Such terse, opaque coding is a great way to shore up your own long-term employment prospects since it is so difficult to make sense of the code. But I would submit that the “long way” is materially no less efficient for computers, and is equally or more efficient for groups of programmers who have to collaborate on coding projects. It does mean more typing, of course, but so much less difficulty troubleshooting!</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com"> 
             <div class="reply"></div> 
             <div class="rating__container clearfix" aria-live="polite">

             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/2d9d15074d11bb563afa30457b62a739?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/2d9d15074d11bb563afa30457b62a739?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>JJ</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190411" title="Jump to Commentlink #135">June 9, 2010 6:20 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>I have been coding JS since Brendan Eich was coding the first version (I used to talk to him regulary as well when I was writing my own books on Internet programming.)Shortening and optimising JavaScript to make it do essentially the same thing is useful, but it’s not the be-all and end-all of “web development”. Any techniques that work and are robust are fine. There’s nothing new here; I wrote about these techniques 15 years ago.</p> 
             <p>Smashing worries a bit too much about the “look” of things when any successful web developer will tell you it’s the bigger picture that matters so much more. Do you design profitable websites that people love to use? I do, and have been doing so for over 15 years – my websites use HTML tables, deprecated JavaScript and even IFRAMES (!) and they all work, they are all secure, and they are all highly profitable.</p> 
             <p>Remember, bigger picture, people…</p> 
            </div>

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">136</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190412"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/cf11b026211a14ae41613d83850f2a62?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/cf11b026211a14ae41613d83850f2a62?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>Alex Kloss</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190412" title="Jump to Commentlink #136">June 27, 2010 3:43 pm</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>Pretty basic stuff. What I missed is a simple pattern for implicit JS object orientation:</p> 
             <p>function obj(options) {<br> if (!options) { return; }<br> var heir = new obj();<br> heir.options = options;<br> heir.init();<br> return heir;<br> }<br> obj.prototype={<br> init: function() { … }<br> };</p> 
             <p>This way, every call of “obj({…})” returns a freshly spawned object that will encapsulate its own state and scope.</p> 
             <p>Another thing I missed is the great flexibility (and speed) of .replace(RegExp, Function) statements.</p> 
             <p>Oh, and I almost forgot closures and the the .call and .apply methods.</p> 
             <p>Other than that, nice article.</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com">

          <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix"> <span class="cn">142</span> 
           <div class="c" id="comment-190414"> 
            <div class="cau clearfix"> 
             <img src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/5a78b2fca9c7bf0e7afb63d85e33a04c?s=38&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" data-src-retina="https://secure.gravatar.com/avatar/5a78b2fca9c7bf0e7afb63d85e33a04c?s=76&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g" class="avatar" height="38" width="38"> 
             <div class="aum"> 
              <h3>jim</h3> 
              <span class="dati"><a href="https://www.smashingmagazine.com/2010/04/seven-javascript-things-i-wish-i-knew-much-earlier-in-my-career/#comment-190414" title="Jump to Commentlink #142">August 20, 2011 7:23 am</a></span> 
             </div> 
            </div> 
            <div class="ctx"> 
             <p>I use JavaScript to make a living. I have for a long time with small companies in the midwest, mid-sized companies in NYC, and major companies in the valley. Through this whole time, I must say you are the odd person out. It’s not what is right or wrong. It’s all about working on a team, and what is agreed on so the team members individually can all reach their max productivity.</p> 
             <p>Spaces vs. tabs work better outside of GUIs, and believe me, A LOT people (me NOT included), use vi, vim, emacs, and a few other command line editors. Tabs make their lives a living hell, so I respect their wishes and use spaces.</p> 
             <p>Ternary operation exists for a reason. People like it. Many people will agree in the real world.</p> 
             <p>Overall, my advice is to get over that syntax hump. Read a lot of code, and work with a variety of people on numerous diverse projects. You will understand the “why” in a lot of this, get used to certain things, and your tune will change overall. Pretty soon you might even be minimizing semicolons, and putting commas at the beginning of line breaks (which would put a smile on my face).</p> 
             <p>As for the article itself, I give it a 5.5 out of 10. It’s for beginners, uses some terms incorrectly (which I am also guilty of sometimes), but overall misses very basic important concepts. The major one of these is event propagation, which was already mentioned in the comments.</p> 
            </div> 
            <div class="com">
